Carolina Lanes, Inc. v. Los Angeles


Civ. No. 31150


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


253 Cal. App. 2d 831; 61 Cal. Rptr. 630; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2410



August 24, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Thomas C. Yager, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief and for an injunction to restrain a city, and certain of its officials, from enforcing rules adopted by the city's board of police commissioners regulating burlesque and strip-tease entertainment.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying relief affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporations sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which determined that Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code, Ordinance §§ 103.102, 103.27, regulating burlesque and strip-tease entertainment, were constitutional and refused to restrain respondents, a city, its board of police commissioners, and its chief of police, from enforcing the rules.


OVERVIEW: One corporation owned the premises on which there was a bowling alley, and the other corporation operated a restaurant and a cocktail lounge on the premises. The trial court found that the corporations had joint ownership and management. The cocktail lounge was set up like a nightclub, and at times, it could be viewed from the bowling alley. The cocktail lounge had strip-tease entertainment at lunch. The entertainment was discontinued after the board threatened to enforce its rules regarding strip-tease entertainment in connection with bowling alleys. On appeal, the court affirmed the constitutionality of the rules. The court held that the purpose of Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 103.102 and the associated board rules was to safeguard the peace, health, safety, convenience, morals, and welfare of the public, including the minors who were attracted to the bowling alley. The rules were not arbitrary and did not constitute an unreasonable classification. The rules were not vague or indefinite.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's determination that the rules regulating burlesque and strip-tease entertainment were constitutional and enforceable.

Patterson v. Sharp


Civ. No. 31012


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


253 Cal. App. 2d 838; 61 Cal. Rptr. 517; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2411



August 24, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Alfred Gitelson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a county clerk to issue subpoenas to certain city police officers to appear as witnesses in a pending action for damages for personal injuries.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgment affirmed insofar as it directs issuance of subpoenas, reversed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner litigant sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondent county clerk to issue subpoenas to police officers to appear as witnesses on the litigant's behalf in an action for personal injuries. The clerk did not issue the subpoenas because the litigant did not pay the fee required under Cal. Gov't Code § 68097.2. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) directed the issuance of the subpoenas. The clerk appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that Cal. Gov't Code §§ 68097.2 and 68097.4-68097.5 were unconstitutional. The court found it unnecessary to reach the question of the constitutionality of the legislation because it found that Cal. Gov't Code §§ 68097-68097.8 was never intended to reach the type of situation where the governmental agency involved was itself one of the actual defendants in the case. Accordingly, the court found that the usual statutory provisions in respect of witnesses were applicable. The court affirmed the judgment insofar as it required the issuance of subpoenas to the named officers, but reversed the judgment in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment insofar as it required the issuance of subpoenas, but reversed the judgment in all other respects.

In re Spinks


Crim. No. 2844


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


253 Cal. App. 2d 748; 61 Cal. Rptr. 743; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2401



August 21, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 19, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County discharging petitioner from custody in a county jail. George A. Lazar, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which discharged defendant from custody in a county jail after issuing a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to a charge of disorderly conduct. Thereafter, defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus. The trial court issued the writ and released defendant from the county jail. The state appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the writ of habeas corpus. The court found that it was not unconstitutional to punish a chronic alcoholic with disorderly conduct because the statute did not punish a chronic alcoholic because of his chronic alcoholism, but punished him because he appeared in a public place while intoxicated. Therefore, the court reversed the order of discharge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order releasing defendant from the county jail after he pleaded guilty to disorderly conduct.

County of Los Angeles v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


Civ. No. 31619


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


253 Cal. App. 2d 670; 62 Cal. Rptr. 435; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2393



August 18, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of the Real Party in Interest for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 11, 1967. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County from issuing a preliminary injunction and from taking any further proceedings in a pending action.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the city, county, police commissioner and chief of police, filed a writ in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), from issuing a preliminary injunction and from taking further action against them in an action brought by plaintiff real party in interest. Defendants contend that the trial court lacked jurisdiction and plaintiff lacked the standing to bring the cause of action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff real party in interest as a taxpayer and resident filed an action pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a, to enjoin defendants, city, county, police commissioner, and chief of police, from the illegal expenditure of public funds and property of the city and of the county. Plaintiff alleged that defendants while on public duty, but beyond the scope of their employment, had made out of court statements and provided, out of court, information to representatives of news media about persons who had just been arrested and about defendants facing trial in pending criminal cases, based on sources not open to the public. Defendants contended that respondent trial court lacked jurisdiction, and that plaintiff lacked standing to bring a request for relief. The court issued the writ of prohibition requiring respondent court to refrain from issuing the preliminary injunction because the court held that while the trial court had jurisdiction and plaintiff had standing, the trial court lacked the power to issue the proposed injunction as it would unconstitutionally abridge freedom of speech or freedom of the press.


OUTCOME: The court issued the writ of prohibition holding that respondent trial court lacked the authority to grant the relief sought because such an injunction would unconstitutionally abridge freedom of speech and freedom of the press. The court found that the trial court had jurisdiction and plaintiff as a taxpayer had standing to bring the cause of action.

 

1239 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pineda


Crim. No. 5832


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


253 Cal. App. 2d 443; 62 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2367



August 11, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 5, 1967. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Raymond G. Callaghan, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of a narcotic.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Santa Clara County refused to grant defendant any relief on his motion for a new trial, made following his conviction for possession of a narcotic, reversal of his sentence by the appellate court, and a remand for sentencing, on the ground that the state of the proceedings was such that it did not have the authority to entertain a motion for a new trial. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's sentence was reversed because a prior misdemeanor conviction had been treated as a felony during sentencing. On remand, defendant sought a new trial. On appeal, the court held that the affirmance of the issue of guilt on the first appeal precluded the trial court's subsequent review of the issue on a motion for a new trial. The court further held that the scope of the appeal was limited to matters arising in connection with the second sentencing, that the question of unlawfully seized evidence could not be raised on collateral attack, and that defendant did not excuse his failure to raise the objection to the search warrant on his prior appeal. Finally, the court held that defense counsel's failure to question the issuance of the search warrant and to object to the evidence produced thereby was not ineffective assistance. An error in the judgment of whether an affidavit for a search warrant was legally and factually sufficient did not evidence such lack of diligence or competence as to reduce the trial to a farce or sham and the inadequacy of the search warrant did not preclude the use of evidence if other evidence established probable cause for the search.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court.

 

1240 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Glenbrook Development Co. v. Brea


Civ. No. 8246


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


253 Cal. App. 2d 267; 61 Cal. Rptr. 189; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2347



August 7, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 30, 1967. The Petition of Respondent City for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 5, 1967, with the Statement, "We do not Understand the Opinion of the Court of Appeal to have Decided that the Issue of Similarity Should not be Tried." Tobriner, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Warren J. Ferguson, Judge.

Action by a subdivider for declaratory relief to determine whether a city water system or a county water district is entitled to serve a subdivision tract, and for an adjudication as to the validity of conditions imposed on the tract by the city; cross-actions by both city and county water district for declaratory relief.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment for city declaring conditions valid reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer brought an action against defendants, city water department and county water district, to determine whether the city or county controlled water service to the development. The Superior Court of Orange County (California) ruled for the city. The county appealed.


OVERVIEW: The city advised the developer that it had to perform certain conditions to comply with its ordinance, Brea, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.74, which stated that a subdivision's domestic water supply within the city limits was to be provided by the city. The county, however, already had rights to provide the water service due to its general obligation bonded indebtedness. The trial court found for the city in the developer's action to determine whether the city or county controlled water service to the development. The court reversed the decision and held that the county was to provide water to the subdivision. The court held that the city was a "publicly owned utility" as defined in Cal. Water Code § 31053 because it served the public. Also, the county was a municipal corporation. The court held that § 31053 was constitutionally valid under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 19. As a result, the county, and not the city, had the legal obligation to supply water. Therefore, the city's conditions were invalid, and it was not permitted to provide water to the subdivision where that obligation was held by the county unless the city complied with § 31053.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision for the city and ruled for the county in the determination of which entity was to provide water to the developer's subdivision.

People v. Rehman


Crim. No. 9534


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


253 Cal. App. 2d 119; 61 Cal. Rptr. 65; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2326



August 2, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 31, 1967, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 27, 1967. Mosk, J., did not Participate Therein. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.

Prosecution for conspiracy in violation of Pen. Code, § 182.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted under Cal. Penal Code § 182 of conspiracy to commit acts injurious to the public health, including practicing medicine without a license. Defendants appealed the convictions and denial of a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendants contended that certain statutory provisions were so unconstitutionally vague as to fail as a definition of criminal conduct. Penal Code § 1150, (3) the corporate documents were properly admitted into evidence, (4) prosecution expert testimony was properly received, (5) proof of fraud was not essential to establish the conspiracy to commit acts injurious to the public health, and (6) there was no basis for finding that defendants were not given a speedy and fair trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions and denial of new trials.

Sehgal v. Knight


Civ. No. 11450


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


253 Cal. App. 2d 170; 61 Cal. Rptr. 118; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2328



August 2, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Yuba County. Richard A. Schoenig, Judge.

Action for damages for personal injuries sustained by pedestrian struck at or near a signal-controlled intersection.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff pedestrian sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Yuba County (California) in favor of defendant driver in an action for damages for personal injuries sustained by the pedestrian struck at or near a signal-controlled intersection.


OVERVIEW: The pedestrian testified that he commenced to walk across a highway, that he complied with a green traffic light, and that he was struck by an automobile. The driver testified that she had been proceeding on a green light. Several witnesses testified that the pedestrian had been outside the crosswalk when he was struck. The issue of the pedestrian's contributory negligence and his possible "jaywalking" were discussed in the jury arguments of both counsel. The pedestrian contended that the trial court erred in reading to the jury Marysville, Cal., Ordinance No. 678 because he claimed that it conflicted with provisions of the California Vehicle Code, which preempted the field. The city ordinance was an outright prohibition against crossing streets outside of crosswalks. The court held that the ordinance was fully within the terms of the authority conferred by Cal. Veh. Code § 21961 and found no validity in it. The court found that the jury instructions were in conflict. Since the prohibitory ordinance applied, the trial court should not have instructed the jury on the permissive jaywalking rule of Cal. Veh. Code § 21954(a). However, the court found no prejudicial error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the trial court.

People v. Fritz


Crim. No. 11160


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


253 Cal. App. 2d 7; 61 Cal. Rptr. 247; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2314



July 31, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petitions for a Hearing by the Supreme Court were Denied September 27, 1967. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Joseph A. Sprankle, Jr., Judge.

Prosecutions for attempted robbery, assault with intent to commit murder and first degree robbery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of the judgments from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting defendants of attempted robbery under Cal. Penal Code §§ 664, 211, assault with intent to commit murder under Cal. Penal Code § 217, and robbery of the first degree under Cal. Penal Code § 211a, in an action prosecuted by the State.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted for attempted robbery, assault with intent to commit murder, and robbery of the first degree. On review, defendants argued that their arrest was unlawful and that the search and seizure of two handguns were unconstitutional. The court held that the arresting officers had reasonable and probable cause for the warrantless arrest of defendants. It was found that the arresting officers presumably knew at the time they arrived to arrest defendants that defendants had committed armed robbery and that one of them had used his hand gun in the robbery and wounded one of the victims. Thus, the court concluded that the circumstances warranted the arresting officers' noncompliance with the prior demand and explanation provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 844. Additionally, the court found that at the trial defendants did not object to the arrest and search on the ground that it was unconstitutional and therefore the court could not consider the argument on appeal. The court held that all searches of a suspect's car, other than at the immediate time and in the immediate vicinity of his arrest, were not ipso facto constitutionally unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' conviction for attempted robbery, assault with intent to commit murder, and robbery of the first degree.

California Water & Telephone Co. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 29880


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


253 Cal. App. 2d 16; 61 Cal. Rptr. 618; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2315



July 31, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Walter H. Odemar, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief to determine whether a county water ordinance can constitutionally apply to investor-owned public utilities and to enjoin the county and the county engineer from enforcing the ordinance against the public utilities.



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed. Judgment for plaintiffs modified and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, investor-owned public utilities, brought an action against defendants, county and county engineer, seeking a declaration that the Water Ordinance, Los Angeles County, Cal., Ordinance 7834, was unconstitutional as applied to them. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County declared the ordinance unconstitutional as applied to the utilities and issued an injunction against its enforcement. The county and the county engineer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The utilities alleged that the Water Ordinance, Los Angeles County, Cal., Ordinance 7834, did not constitutionally apply to them because the state had fully occupied the field of public utilities regulation by adopting Cal. Const. art. XII, § 23 and the Public Utilities Code. At all times the utilities had complied with the provisions of the ordinance, and there had been no prosecution of any of the utilities. Therefore, the county argued that the case was not justiciable. The court held that (1) the controversy was justiciable; (2) the ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to the utilities because it conflicted with the general law and related to matters which were of state-wide rather than local concern; and (3) the issuance of an ancillary injunction was improper because there was no reason to suppose that either the county or the county engineer was in the least disposed to enforce the ordinance against the utilities in the teeth of a declaration of its unconstitutionality as applied to them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision finding that the water ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to the utilities. The court modified the judgment of the trial court by striking the provision therefrom enjoining the county and the county engineer from enforcing the ordinance against the utilities.

 

1245 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Web Service Co. v. Spencer


Civ. No. 8526


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


252 Cal. App. 2d 827; 61 Cal. Rptr. 493; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1574



July 25, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 8, 1967, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 21, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Stephen K. Tamura, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the treasurer and license collector of a city to issue a business license to a service company.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment granting writ reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city treasurer sought review after the Superior Court of Orange County (California) ordered the treasurer to issue a business license to petitioner service company. The service company had filed a proceeding in mandamus to compel the treasurer to issue a license based on one location. The service company challenged the constitutionality of Anaheim, Cal., Mun. Code § 3.08.010 as applied to route operators and laundromat operators.


OVERVIEW: The service company furnished and maintained coin-operated laundry machines located in wash rooms at apartment houses, motels, and trailer courts. The person operating such a business was known as a "route operator." The service company tendered an amount computed under the gross receipts provisions of the ordinance contending that it was required to obtain one license; contended that the provisions requiring a separate license for each "branch establishment or location" did not apply to route operators; and demanded a license. The treasurer contended that the service company had 261 locations, each requiring a license. The trial court ordered the city to issue a license. The court reversed. Each washroom location was a business location within the meaning of the ordinance. The sections of the ordinance under review were directed to the taxable event of doing business in the City of Anaheim; met the requirement that the classification or measure of tax used be reasonably related to the objective of the legislation; did not unlawfully discriminate against the route operator; and did not impose a tax in an amount that was confiscatory or as a subterfuge to eliminate competition.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that ordered the treasurer to issue a license to the service company based on a single location.

Melton v. San Pablo


Civ. No. 23681


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


252 Cal. App. 2d 794; 61 Cal. Rptr. 29; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1570



July 25, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Homer W. Patterson, Judge.

Action for declaratory relief to determine the constitutionality of a retroactive zoning ordinance requiring all portable or temporary vending establishments to obtain a use permit.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment determining that plaintiff's use of his property was not a pre-existing lawful nonconforming use, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) entered judgment in favor of defendant municipality in plaintiff landowner's action seeking declaratory relief and challenging the constitutionality of a zoning ordinance. The landowner appealed.


OVERVIEW: A landowner began operation of a short-order restaurant in a stationary bus on his property, which was zoned for commercial use. Thereafter, the municipality adopted an amendment to its zoning ordinance. The landowner applied for a use permit under the ordinance. After his permit expired, he filed an action against the municipality, challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance. The trial court found that the landowner was in violation of the municipality's building, electrical, and plumbing codes, and concluded that the ordinance was constitutional. On appeal, the court found that the ordinance was designed to regulate various businesses, including mobile vending establishments. The court also found that the ordinance was based on public health and safety considerations, such as preventing unsafe or dangerous uses of property. The court concluded that the ordinance was not an arbitrary and unreasonable exercise of the municipality's police power. The ordinance bore a reasonable relationship to the proper purposes of zoning. Therefore, the court held that the ordinance was constitutional. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment, which entered judgment in favor of the municipality, was affirmed.

Bean v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 29886


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


252 Cal. App. 2d 754; 60 Cal. Rptr. 804; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1565



July 24, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 21, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Carlos M. Teran, Judge.

Action for relief from a bail bond forfeiture.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to first amended complaint was sustained without leave to amend affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, principal, surety, and its broker, appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained defendant county's demurrer and dismissed plaintiffs' action for relief from a bail bond forfeiture.


OVERVIEW: The principal was charged with misdemeanors in the municipal court and was released upon bail bonds furnished by the surety. The principal pled guilty to the misdemeanors and was ordered to appear for sentencing. When he failed to appear, the bonds were forfeited. Plaintiffs filed an amended complaint in the superior court for relief from the bond forfeiture, and the county filed a demurrer. The superior court sustained the demurrer and dismissed plaintiffs' complaint. Plaintiffs appealed, and the court affirmed the decision of the superior court. The court held that the surety, having breached its obligation under the bond, could not collaterally attack the proceedings in the municipal court, based upon the alleged insanity of the principal. It was not shown that the principal was physically unable by reason of illness or insanity to appear in the municipal court. The court ruled that is was mandatory upon the surety under Cal. Penal Code § 1305 to bring the principal's insanity to the attention of the municipal court within 90 days after the forfeiture was declared in order to have the forfeiture set aside, which the surety failed to do.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court, which sustained the county's demurrer and dismissed plaintiffs' complaint, which asked for relief from a bail bond forfeiture.

People v. Limon


Crim. No. 6113


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


252 Cal. App. 2d 575; 60 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1539



July 14, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order denying a new trial. Benjamin Landis, Judge.

Prosecution for hit and run driving in a personal injury accident.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed; appeal from order dismissed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of hit and run driving in a personal injury accident. Defendant argued that there were errors in the jury instructions and a violation of his right against self-incrimination.


OVERVIEW: Defendant struck a pedestrian while driving his car. A witness attempted to render assistance. When the police arrived, defendant was not present but was found 35 minutes later, walking back in the direction of the accident. Defendant was subsequently convicted of hit and run in a personal injury accident. On appeal, defendant argued that there were errors in the jury instructions and a violation of his right against self-incrimination. The court disagreed and affirmed the conviction. The court first held that the trial court was not required to instruct the jury that defendant was not required to render assistance if the victim was being adequately cared for by others. The court held that omission to perform any of the acts required in the statute constituted the offense. In any case, the court held that failure to give such an instruction did not prejudice defendant's case. The court held that there was ample reason for the jury to conclude that defendant did not engage in the process of helping. The court then held that defendant's right against self-incrimination was not violated merely because he was required to provide his name at the scene.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

1249 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morales


Crim. No. 5784


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


252 Cal. App. 2d 537; 60 Cal. Rptr. 671; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1534



July 13, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 7, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County. Joseph G. Wilson, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of a sharp instrument by a person confined in a state prison.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions as to sentence imposed and affirmed in other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which convicted him of possession of a sharp instrument by a person confined in a state prison in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4502. Defendant was sentenced to a consecutive rather than a concurrent prison sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of a sharp instrument by a person confined in a state prison in violation of § 4502, and the trial court imposed a consecutive as opposed to a concurrent prison sentence. Defendant alleged that § 4502 was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction but reversed and remanded defendant's sentence. The court held that § 4502 was constitutional because it sufficiently apprised defendant of the action that constituted a violation of the statute. But the court held that the trial court abused its discretion by imposing the consecutive sentence upon defendant because the sentence was imposed because defendant plead not guilty to the charge. Finally, the court held that the trial court erred by penalizing defendant for asserting his constitutional right to have plaintiff prove his guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed and remanded in part an order of the trial court, which convicted defendant of possession of a sharp instrument by a person confined in a state prison, and he was sentenced to a consecutive rather than a concurrent prison sentence.

People v. Norman


Crim. No. 5367


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


252 Cal. App. 2d 381; 60 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1514

PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Carl H. Allen, Judge.

Prosecution for burglary.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction of second degree burglary affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) convicted defendant of burglary in the second degree in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 459-60. Defendant was thereafter sentenced to a prison term. Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that he was arrested without probable cause and that the illegality of his arrest tainted the search and seizure. The court found no merit to defendant's objections to his arrest and held that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress. The court held that defendant failed to show that the amount of bail fixed upon the indictment was determined in an erroneous manner or that it was excessive. Further, the court held that defendant failed to demonstrate that the trial court abused its discretion in the denial of his motion to reduce bail, or that the rulings on the matter of bail adversely affected the outcome of the case. The court also found that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion for the appointment of an alternate attorney, and committed no error in granting defendant's motion to appear in propria persona. The court concluded that defendant was not deprived of any constitutional right to counsel or due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

People v. Crosslin


Crim. No. 5405


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


251 Cal. App. 2d 968; 60 Cal. Rptr. 309; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2061



June 22, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 14, 1967, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 20, 1967. * Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County. Frank W. Rose, Judge.

Prosecution for armed robbery, attempted armed robbery and assault with a deadly weapon.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of conviction for robbery and attempted robbery from the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California).


OVERVIEW: The defendant was found guilty of armed robbery at a market and attempted armed robbery and assault with a deadly weapon at a liquor store. On appeal, the court held that the trial court properly admitted into evidence: (1) a card containing defendant's fingerprints as a business record under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1953f, even though the person who actually took defendant's fingerprints and made the card was not called as a witness, because the card was verified as authentic; (2) defendant's statements after his arrest and prior to his being advised of his rights to counsel and to remain silent that were used for the purpose of obtaining a voice identification because they did not elicit incriminating information; (3) statements made by defendant in which he falsely identified himself to the police; and (4) several articles taken from defendant's apartment at the time of his arrest because there was a nexus between the items seized and defendant's criminal behavior. The court also concluded that defendant's convictions for attempted armed robbery and assault with a deadly weapon were supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction for robbery and attempted robbery.

 

1252 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Toole v. Richardson-Merrell, Inc.


Civ. No. 23181


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


251 Cal. App. 2d 689; 60 Cal. Rptr. 398; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2025; 29 A.L.R.3d 988



June 12, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied 251 Cal. App. 2d 689 at 718.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Walter Carpeneti, Judge.

Action for general and punitive damages for injuries suffered as a result of the use of a drug manufactured and marketed by defendant and prescribed by plaintiffs' physician.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) entered judgment on a jury verdict, which awarded compensatory and punitive damages against appellant drug manufacturer and in favor of respondent patient, in the patient's action which alleged that he developed serious vision problems as a result of the drug's toxic qualities. The drug manufacturer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The manufacturer knew that tests of its drug on various animals had resulted in, inter alia, abnormal blood changes and serious eye damage, but concealed the negative results and falsified positive test results, leading to Food and Drug Administration (FDA) approval of the drug. The court held that the trial court properly instructed the jury that a violation of § 355(b) of the Federal Food, Drug, and Cosmetic Act, which required submission of reports of investigations, created a rebuttable presumption of negligence. The trial court properly refused to instruct the jury that § 355(b) required anything less than submission of all relevant data which bore upon the safety of the drug. The manufacturer's statements regarding the safety of the drug, even if only expressions of scientific opinion, supported an action for fraud and deceit, because it had superior knowledge regarding the subject matter of the representations, and the patient and his physician reasonably relied on that knowledge. Finally, the court properly instructed the jury on the issue of implied warranty, because the drug had been placed upon the market and sold without adequate and proper warning.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which awarded compensatory and punitive damages to the patient.

 

1253 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weger


Crim. No. 13090


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


251 Cal. App. 2d 584; 59 Cal. Rptr. 661; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2010



June 5, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 26, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Municipal Court for the Pasadena Judicial District of Los Angeles County dismissing a prosecution for disorderly conduct after a demurrer to the complaint was sustained. Louis T. Fletcher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged an order of the Municipal Court for the Pasadena Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1008, sustaining a demurrer and dismissing a prosecution against defendant for a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647 and filed a demurrer. The trial court sustained the demurrer and dismissed the prosecution pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1008, finding Cal. Penal Code § 647 to be unconstitutional. The state sought review, and the court reversed the order. The court held that § 647 was not unconstitutionally vague. The statute was entitled to a presumption of constitutionality and was not vague merely because it gave police discretion in deciding when to temporarily detain persons for questioning. Detaining persons on the street for questioning was permissible short of probable cause as long as the officers had at least a reasonable suspicion. Also, Cal. Penal Code § 647(e) did not violate the self-incrimination privilege because asking a person to produce identification did not constitute self-incrimination. The court held that § 647(e) struck a balance between a person's interest in immunity from police interference and the community's interest in law enforcement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing the state's prosecution of defendant.

People v. Donohue


Crim. No. 6133


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


251 Cal. App. 2d 272; 59 Cal. Rptr. 417; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1969



May 23, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. John G. Barnes, Judge.

Prosecution for sale of amphetamine, a dangerous drug.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found him guilty of violating of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11912 in connection with the sale of amphetamine.


OVERVIEW: A drug agent met defendant in a bar. They conversed briefly and the agent followed defendant in his car until defendant pulled over. The agent asked defendant how much he wanted for a jar of amphetamine and the two agreed on a price and a quantity. Defendant removed the drugs from his trunk, handed the drugs to the agent, and accepted the money the agent gave him. Defendant was convicted for selling a dangerous drug to the agent and challenged his conviction, claiming that there was no proof that amphetamine was a dangerous drug. The court affirmed his conviction, finding that the legislature has expressly defined the term "restricted dangerous drugs" to include the substance known to pharmacology as "amphetamine." Accordingly, the information appropriately charged that defendant sold "a dangerous drug, to wit, amphetamine" and evidence that defendant sold to the agent a quantity of tablets which upon analysis proved to be amphetamine was sufficient to sustain a conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for selling a dangerous drug.

 

1256 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hohensee


Crim. No. 2426


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


251 Cal. App. 2d 193; 59 Cal. Rptr. 234; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1961



May 19, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 12, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Eli H. Levenson, Judge.

Prosecutions for conspiracy to cheat and defraud and conspiracy to advertise a drug or device represented to have any effect in certain diseases.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged an order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which entered judgments of conviction upon jury verdicts of conspiracy to cheat and defraud and conspiracy to advertise a drug or device represented to have any effect in certain diseases in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182(4) and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 26286.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendants gave health lectures expressing views contrary to accepted medical opinion, during which defendants solicited contributions and sold honey, lucite graters, wrinkle removing cream, tenderizers, and blenders, representing them to be essential to proper health care in order to induce sales. Defendants were convicted of conspiracy to cheat and defraud and conspiracy to advertise a drug or device represented to have any effect in certain diseases. On appeal, the court affirmed. Evidence obtained against defendants by tape recording their public lectures was not obtained by a search or by an unauthorized physical intrusion into a private area. One defendant's statements referring to his prison record tended to establish a general scheme of defrauding by eliciting sympathy and then soliciting and collecting contributions from his elderly victims. There was no error in admitting the evidence. The evidence and the entire scheme supported the finding of intent to defraud. Defendants were convicted of conspiracy. The evil agreement itself was unlawful. Whether or not the agreement was carried out was immaterial to the charge of conspiracy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for conspiracy to cheat and defraud and conspiracy to advertise a drug or device represented to have any effect in certain diseases.

Coates Capitol Corp. v. Superior Court of Alameda County


Civ. No. 24265


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


251 Cal. App. 2d 125; 59 Cal. Rptr. 231; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1954



May 18, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDINGS in prohibition to prevent the Superior Court of Alameda County from proceeding further in an action and in mandamus to compel dismissal of the action as to petitioner.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writs granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner domestic corporation sought a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent, the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) (trial court), from proceeding a wrongful death action brought by real parties in interest decedent's representatives and a writ of mandate ordering dismissal of the case.


OVERVIEW: The decedent's representatives filed a wrongful death action against the domestic corporation. The trial court denied the domestic corporation's motion to dismiss the action for failure to serve and return summons within the period prescribed by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581a. The domestic corporation instituted a proceeding for a writ of prohibition to prevent the trial court from proceeding further in the action and a writ of mandate ordering dismissal of the case. The crucial question was whether the period prescribed by § 581a was tolled because the domestic corporation secreted itself to prevent the service of summons. The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition and a peremptory writ of mandate. The court concluded that the motion to dismiss was meritorious. The court found that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 416.3 did not apply and that the domestic corporation applied for mandate well within the applicable period of limitations. The court held that the domestic corporation could not secrete itself within the state within the meaning of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581a.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition against the trial court. The court also issued a peremptory writ of mandate issue that commanded the trial court to set aside and vacate the order that denied the domestic corporation's motion to dismiss the action brought by the decedent's representatives and to dismiss the action as to the domestic corporation.

People v. Cuevas


Crim. No. 12348


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


250 Cal. App. 2d 901; 59 Cal. Rptr. 6; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2182



May 12, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 5, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Joseph L. Call, Judge.

Prosecution for the illegal possession of heroin for sale.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions. That part of judgment of conviction finding prior convictions and imposing sentences therefor reversed with directions; in all other respects judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of illegal possession of heroin for sale in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of illegal possession of heroin for sale in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.5. Defendant sought review and contended that the search, which led to the discovery of the narcotics, was illegal. The court held that, in determining whether or not the police conducted a legal search of defendant's vehicle, any factual conflict was impliedly resolved in favor of the State. The court found that, because the police had the requisite probable cause to arrest defendant, the fact that the search preceded the arrest was immaterial because the two acts were substantially contemporaneous. The court held that defendant failed to prove his burden that his allegation of inadequate representations was not a matter of speculation but was a demonstrable reality. The court affirmed the illegal possession conviction. However, the court found that evidence of defendant's prior convictions were not properly authenticated under Cal. Penal Code § 969b and Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1905, which required that the documents be certified. The court remanded for sentencing. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the judgment, which convicted defendant of illegal possession. The court reversed in part the judgment, which sentenced defendant based upon prior convictions because the trial court failed to properly authenticate those prior judgments of conviction.

Cohen v. Municipal Court of Los Angeles Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 29558


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


250 Cal. App. 2d 861; 58 Cal. Rptr. 846; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2177



May 10, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 7, 1967. Kingsley, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 5, 1967. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County denying an alternative writ of prohibition. Harold P. Huls, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denying an alternative writ of prohibition after he was convicted of wilfully, unlawfully, and knowingly preparing, exhibiting, and offering to exhibit obscene matter in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 311.2.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with wilfully, unlawfully, and knowingly preparing, exhibiting, and offering to exhibit obscene matter in violation of § 311.2. He filed a demurrer to the complaint, which was overruled by the trial court. He petitioned for an alternative writ of prohibition. The petition was denied. On appeal, defendant's main contention was that due process required that the specific matter alleged to be obscene must have been pleaded. The court affirmed the denial of the petition. The court held that under both statutory and case law it was sufficient to charge the offense in the language of the statute. It was therefore not necessary to plead the specific material alleged to be obscene.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that denied defendant's petition for an alternative writ of prohibition.

Gong v. Fremont


Civ. No. 23308


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


250 Cal. App. 2d 568; 58 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2139



May 1, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from portions of a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Lionel J. Wilson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel a city to annul two conditions attached to a land use permit.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment granting writ reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which refused to uphold the validity of a condition attached to appellee landowners' land use permit.


OVERVIEW: The landowners applied to the city planning commission for a use permit to build a gas station. The permit was issued with several conditions. The landowners sought review by the city council, which modified two of the conditions and denied the appeal as to two others. The landowners petitioned the trial court for a writ of mandamus to annul the two unmodified conditions. The trial court overruled the city's general demurrer and commanded the city to set aside one of the conditions. The city appealed and the court reversed. The court held that the petition was not sufficient to have withstood the general demurrer because the facts alleged failed to show the landowners' entitlement to relief. The court found that the landowners could have been entitled to relief under either traditional mandamus or administrative mandamus. The court said that the first did not apply because the landowners had not sought the enforcement of purely ministerial duties. The court held that the second did not apply because the landowners had not shown that the city's actions were in excess of its jurisdiction, that there was not a fair trial of factual matters, or that there was an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a refusal to uphold the validity of a condition attached by the city to the landowners' land use permit.
