Holtzendorff v. Housing Authority of Los Angeles


Civ. Nos. 29865, 29866


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


250 Cal. App. 2d 596; 58 Cal. Rptr. 886; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2144



May 1, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 18, 1967, and the Petition of Defendant and Appellant and Cross-complainant and Respondent for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 28, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Leonard A. Diether, Judge.

Action to recover damages for wrongful discharge under employment contracts and cross-complaint to recover sums paid as reimbursement for attorney fees and defense expenses.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments for plaintiffs and for cross-complainant affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff public employees brought an action for wrongful discharge against defendant housing authority. The housing authority filed a counterclaim against one employee for money paid to him as reimbursement for legal fees. The jury in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) found for employees on their discharge claim and for the authority on its counterclaim. All parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court upheld the employment contracts, because the authority had the power to give fixed terms of employment, the contracts delegated administrative duties only, and the compensation was not a gift forbidden by Cal. Const. art. IV, §§ 31, 32. The contracts were binding upon the authority beyond the term of the commissioners who entered the contracts. Therefore, employees could be discharged only for good cause, which was an issue of fact for the jury. The jury properly found that a secret monitoring system to prevent communist infiltration was installed in good faith to further the best interests of the authority and did not justify discharge of employee who installed it. Employee's compelled testimony was not grounds for discharge. On the authority's counterclaim, the court held that the authority could not assume the legal expense of its agents in these circumstances. Cal. Gov't Code § 1956. The applicable statute of limitations was the three-year statute in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 338(4), governing an action for relief on the ground of mistake, not the two-year statute governing actions for money had and received. Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 339.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed both judgments.

 

1262 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jenkins


Crim. No. 11482


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


250 Cal. App. 2d 460; 58 Cal. Rptr. 401; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2126



April 26, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Herbert V. Walker, Judge.

Prosecution for manslaughter.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting defendant of manslaughter and sentencing him to a term of imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict or to support the finding that the allegation as to prior felony conviction was true. Defendant also contended that he was denied due process of law; that he was denied a fair trial by misconduct of the deputy district attorney; and that the trial court erred in receiving in evidence a statement made by defendant to the police. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to support the finding that defendant had been convicted previously of a felony and that he had served a term in prison therefor. The court further held that the admission of evidence of a prior conviction for the purpose of impeachment pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 2051, has been upheld in innumerable decisions. The court also held that defendant was not denied a fair trial in any respect. Finally, the court held that the trial court did not err in receiving defendant's statements in evidence because it was stipulated that there was no objection to the use, at the trial, of the statement made by defendant to officers. Accordingly, the court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of manslaughter and the sentence imposed.

 

1263 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Santin v. Cranston


Civ. No. 11353


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


250 Cal. App. 2d 438; 59 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2123



April 25, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Elvin F. Sheehy, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the State Controller to accede to a decision by the Board of Control allowing larger monthly retirement benefits to retired military personnel.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment denying writ reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner military officers appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which ruled in favor of respondent California State Controller in the officers' action for a writ of mandamus to compel the controller to comply with a decision of the California Board of Control. The board ordered that the officers were entitled to a larger amount of retirement benefits.


OVERVIEW: Shortly before the officers retired, the legislature amended the statute relating to military retirement. The statute, Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code §§ 215, 228, had granted full retirement benefits for officers with 20 years of military service, while the amendment changed the language to include only full-time active military service in the retirement computation. The officers had each served more than 20 years in full time active service, with an additional 2 or 3 years on inactive reserve status. The controller computed the officers' retirement benefits under the amendments. The board ordered that they should receive their benefits under the old calculation, but the controller refused. The trial court denied the officers' petition for a writ of mandamus. On appeal, the court reversed. The court refused to apply the amendments retrospectively. The court found that the officers had a vested right in the benefits as they were under the original law which could not be abolished or impaired, although they could be modified, under Cal. Const. art. I, § 16. The court refused to imply into the statutory language an intent of the legislature to make the retirement rights nonvesting.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying the officers' petition for a writ of mandamus to compel the controller to comply with the board's decision and issued the writ.

 

1264 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Knighton


Crim. No. 2577


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


250 Cal. App. 2d 221; 58 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2097



April 19, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County. Edward P. Fogg, Judge.

Prosecution for issuing a check without sufficient funds and for grand theft.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that convicted him of issuing a check without sufficient funds in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 476(a), and with the unlawful taking of an automobile, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487 (grand theft). The information against defendant had included a charge of a prior conviction of a felony, but the charge was stricken before sentencing.


OVERVIEW: During cross-examination at trial, defendant testified, without objection, about a prior felony conviction. Before sentencing, he sought to withdraw his admission of the prior conviction, contending that the state had to introduce evidence that his constitutional rights had been protected or waived during the trial that resulted in the prior conviction. The state's motion to strike the prior conviction was granted and the issue was moot. On appeal, the court affirmed the order because: the cross-examination of defendant regarding the prior conviction was not a prejudicial error; and as there was no showing that the testimony of the state's chief witness was inherently improbable, the court would not reweigh that evidence. The court found that a defendant who took the stand as a witness could be impeached in the same manner as any other witness. Because the testimony elicited from defendant that he had been previously convicted and served a term in state prison did not violate his federal or state constitutional rights and the trial judge had given a proper instruction to the jury on the jury's use of the evidence, defendant could not raise the issue for the first time on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for issuing a check without sufficient funds and with unlawful taking of an automobile.

 

1265 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Peckham


Crim. No. 11673


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


249 Cal. App. 2d 941; 57 Cal. Rptr. 922; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2302



April 3, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 25, 1967, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 31, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Herbert V. Walker, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with intent to commit rape.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of assault with intent to commit rape in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 220.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of assault with intent to commit rape in violation of § 220. Defendant challenged the conviction on several grounds. On appeal, the court did not find any merit in defendant's contentions, and affirmed his conviction. The court found the evidence was sufficient for supporting the conviction because his statements and acts that he wanted the victim and that he had come to rape her was sufficient for supporting the finding that defendant had the requisite intent. Further, defendant's evidence regarding his intoxication and his impotency were questions of fact that were presented to the jury, which were resolved against defendant. Next, the court found § 220 was not unconstitutionally vague because assault was defined as an unlawful attempt, coupled with a present ability, to commit a violent injury on the person of another. Even if it was assumed that defendant was impotent and incapable of rape, such impotence did not necessarily preclude his committing an assault.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendant of assault with intent to commit rape in violation of the California statute.

 

1266 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Francis v. County of Stanislaus


Civ. No. 715


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


249 Cal. App. 2d 862; 57 Cal. Rptr. 881; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2297



March 30, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County. Ross A. Carkeet, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

Action to enjoin the County of Stanislaus from prosecuting operators of cardrooms for any breach of an ordinance relative to gambling in that county.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff operators of cardrooms appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), which held that the enactment of the ordinance at issue was within the legitimate authority of the legislative branch of the Stanislaus County government and that it was not violative of the federal or state constitution in favor of defendant county, sheriff, and district attorney.


OVERVIEW: The operators of cardrooms brought a suit to restrain defendants from prosecuting them for any breach of an ordinance adopted by vote of the people of Stanislaus County relative to gambling in commercial cardrooms in the County of Stanislaus. The trial court held that the operators were not entitled to judgment, that the enactment of the ordinance was within the legitimate authority of the legislative branch of the Stanislaus County government, and that it was not violative of the federal or state constitution. The operators appealed and the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the ordinance neither purported to overrule nor to modify the action of the legislature concerning certain specified gambling games. The court held that the county, exercising its police power, could enact a proper ordinance to forbid commercial cardrooms while allowing card playing in private homes or in the private quarters of certain organizations, so long as the rooms were not operated for hire.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendants that held the enactment of the ordinance forbidding commercial cardrooms was within the legitimate authority of the legislative branch of the county government and was not violative of the state or federal constitution.

 

1267 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Mains v. Board of Barber Examiners


Civ. No. 11448


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


249 Cal. App. 2d 459; 57 Cal. Rptr. 573; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2244



March 15, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 12, 1967. Regan, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 31, 1967. Traynor, C. J., Burke, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Charles W. Johnson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel Board of Barber Examiners to annul its decision suspending barbershop certificate.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment granting peremptory writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state agency sought review of a judgment entered in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that granted respondent barbershop owner a peremptory writ of mandate that directed the state agency to annul its decision to suspend the barbershop owner's barbershop certificate.


OVERVIEW: The state agency initially suspended the barbershop owner's barbershop certificate but the trial court granted the barbershop owner's request for a peremptory writ directing the state agency to annul its decision to suspend the barbershop owner's barbershop certificate. On appeal, the state agency argued that the words "practicing beauty culture" contained in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6522 meant persons licensed to practice cosmetology. The state agency further argued that the acts of an individual who cut hair as a part of the practice of cosmetology constituted the practice of barbering. The court rejected the state agency's arguments. The court concluded that the individual who cut hair as a part of his practice of cosmetology as permitted by Cal. Bus & Prof. Code § 7321 was not engaged in the practice of barbering, as set forth in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6250. That being the case, the trial court properly concluded that the individual did not engage in the practice of barbering and, thus, the state agency did not have the authority to annul the barbershop owner's barbershop certificate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

1268 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Porter v. Arthur Murray, Inc.


Civ. No. 8415


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


249 Cal. App. 2d 410; 57 Cal. Rptr. 554; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2236



March 13, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 28, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. William P. Mahedy, Judge.

Action to recover for money had and received for breach of contract to third party beneficiary under franchise agreements to further dancing lessons.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, dance studio and its agents, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found in favor of respondent customer in the customer's breach of contract action.


OVERVIEW: A customer entered into numerous contracts with three successive agents of a dance studio. After the third agent declared bankruptcy, thereby rendering the customer's ability to receive dance lessons meaningless, the customer filed suit for breach of contract, alleging that he was a third party beneficiary of the franchise contract between the dance studio and its agents. On appeal, the agents and the studio claimed that the customer did not demand performance prior to suit, that there was no set time for performance, that there existed no actual agency between the studio and its agents, and that there was extensive performance. The court found that despite the contractual terms to the contrary, there existed an agency relationship and that the customer demanded performance by showing up for dance lessons that had been paid for. There was a breach of contract by virtue of the fact that the customer paid his full tuition and was wrongfully excluded from the school.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

 

1269 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Evans


Crim. No. 339


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


249 Cal. App. 2d 254; 57 Cal. Rptr. 276; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2220



March 7, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of Bakersfield Judicial District of Kern County. Marvin E. Ferguson, Judge.

Prosecution of a hotelkeeper for failure to remit to the municipal tax collector public monies collected by him.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to the complaint was sustained affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of an order of the Appellate Department of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which held that its ordinance was unconstitutional. Appellee hotelkeeper was charged with failure to remit monies to the municipal tax collector. The case was certified to the court under Cal. R. Ct. 63(a) to determine whether criminal procedure was available in the absence of a mens rea in tax collection matters.


OVERVIEW: The city had amended its ordinance to lay a tax upon all persons lodging in the city consisting of four percent of the room rent. The court held that this method of tax collection was a legitimate exercise of authority, the monies collected by the hotelkeeper were trust monies and the city's property, and that the hotelkeeper was required to account for them and pay them to the tax collector. The court noted that the question was whether the ordinance authorized a criminal prosecution. The complaint alleged that the hotelkeeper wilfully and unlawfully failed to make the required payments. The court held that in an offense of this kind it was not necessary to have a fraudulent intent, or any intent other than the intent to perform the specified act entirely apart from motive, or evil intention. However, the court held that the attempted conviction for failure to make the payments described in the ordinance was rendered ineffective because the state had taken over the entire field of punishment for a refusal to pay public monies to the proper official. The court held that the city's attempt to impose a different penalty was thus null and void under Cal. Const., art. XI, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the judgment of the district court and sustained the dismissal of the city's complaint, and held that the city's attempt to impose a penalty for the violation of its ordinance was ineffective.

 

1270 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Magan Medical Clinic v. California State Bd. of Medical Examiners


Civ. No. 30980


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


249 Cal. App. 2d 124; 57 Cal. Rptr. 256; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2207



March 3, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALS from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Edward J. Olstyn, Court Commissioner, Judge pro tem.

Action for declaratory relief to interpret Bus. & Prof. Code, § 654, and other sections with reference to doctor-owned pharmacies.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment declaring that Legislature had power to prohibit physicians and surgeons from owning pharmacies affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) held, inter alia, that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 654 (§ 654) (1) applied to a partnership of physicians and surgeons owning a pharmacy, (2) did not apply to a partnership of physicians and surgeons owning stock of a corporation owning a pharmacy, and (3) was constitutional. Plaintiff physicians, surgeons, and medical clinics and defendant state board of medical examiners (board) appealed.


OVERVIEW: The physicians, surgeons, and medical clinics filed a petition with the board seeking a declaration of the construction and validity of amendments to § 654, but the board declined to file the petition and refused to hold a hearing thereon. The physician, surgeons, and medical clinics subsequently filed an action for declaratory relief, seeking a declaration that § 654 did not require a divesting of interests they held in certain pharmacies. On appeal, the physicians, surgeons and medical clinics argued, inter alia, that § 654 was arbitrary and capricious and that the statute should not be applied to partnerships of doctors. The board, on the other hand, argued that § 654 should be applied to corporations in which doctors held stock. The appellate court rejected the parties' contentions and affirmed the trial court's judgment in all respects, holding, inter alia, (1) that § 654 was constitutional, (2) that the statute applied to partnerships of doctors, and (3) that the legislature did not intend for the statute to apply to corporations in which doctors held stock.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in all respects.

 

1271 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Sarac v. State Board of Education


Civ. No. 29143


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


249 Cal. App. 2d 58; 57 Cal. Rptr. 69; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 2198



March 1, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Macklin Fleming, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the State Board of Education to rescind its revocation of plaintiff's general secondary teaching credential.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied his petition for a writ of mandate against respondent state board of education compelling it to rescind its revocation of his teaching credential.


OVERVIEW: After the teacher was arrested for a homosexual incident, he refrained from contesting a charge of disorderly conduct in exchange for the dismissal of a more serous charge. The board of education revoked his teaching credential on the ground that he had engaged in immoral and unprofessional conduct within the meaning of Cal. Educ. Code § 13202. The trial court denied the teacher's petition for a writ of mandate, and on appeal, the court affirmed. The teacher was not prejudiced in the trial court by the board's erroneous finding that he teacher's conviction had been based on a plea of guilt. Thus, the error could not be used as a basis for reversal. The testimony of witnesses constituted more than ample credible and competent evidence that the teacher committed a homosexual act. There was an obvious rational connection between the teacher's homosexual conduct and the consequent action of the board in revoking the credential on the statutory ground of immoral and unprofessional conduct and evident unfitness for service in the school system. The board had not abused its discretion in the penalty imposed nor were there any constitutional questions with respect to the board's actions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that denied the teacher's petition for a writ of mandate against the state board of education seeking to compel it to rescind the revocation of his teaching credential.

 

1272 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Goldberg v. Regents of University of Cal.


Civ. No. 23556


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


248 Cal. App. 2d 867; 57 Cal. Rptr. 463; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1699



February 28, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 26, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. William H. Brailsford, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Regents of the University of California to reinstate several students suspended or dismissed for violating regulations governing student conduct.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer was sustained without leave to amend affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff students challenged, upon constitutional grounds, their suspension and dismissal from a university. They appealed from the judgment of dismissal of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) entered on an order sustaining the general demurrer of defendant university regents, without leave to amend, to plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandate seeking reinstatement to the university.


OVERVIEW: The students were suspended or dismissed after participating in rallies to protest the arrest of a nonstudent who had displayed on campus a sign reading: "Fuck! Verb." On appeal, the court held that the university's disciplinary action was a proper exercise of its inherent general powers to maintain order on the campus and to exclude those detrimental to its well being. The university' disciplinary procedure did not deprive the students of procedural due process. The students were not deprived of procedural due process when the disciplinary committee did not follow the rules of evidence usually applicable in judicial proceedings and chose not to recognize the privilege against self-incrimination. The committee's failure to hear the tape of the language used by the students in the context of their rally did not infringe on the students' rights to procedural due process. The record indicated that whatever their personal reactions, the acting chancellor and president of the university did everything in their power to perfect and assure the students' rights of procedural due process. The university was not required to await the outcome of criminal proceedings against the students.


OUTCOME: The judgment against the students was affirmed.

Whitlow v. Board of Medical Examiners


Civ. No. 28946


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


248 Cal. App. 2d 478; 56 Cal. Rptr. 525; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1651



February 10, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 6, 1967, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 5, 1967. Mosk, J., did not Participate Therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Alfred Gitelson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Board of Medical Examiners to annul an order suspending plaintiff's certificate for the practice of medicine and surgery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff doctor appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in a proceeding in mandamus to compel defendant Board of Medical Examiners to annul an order suspending the doctor's certificate for the practice of medicine and surgery.


OVERVIEW: The board acted pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 2360, 2361 against the doctor for unprofessional conduct in the issuance of prescriptions for dangerous drugs in violation of § 2399.5, without either prior examination of the patient or medical indication thereof. On appeal, the doctor contended that § 2399.5 was confusing, probably meaningless and, therefore, unenforceable. The court held that the language used in the statute was sufficiently definite to provide a satisfactory standard of conduct for the medical profession whose activities it was designed to regulate and that it was not subject to the constitutional attack leveled at it by the doctor. The doctor also contended that he was entrapped by the agents of the board into issuing the prescriptions. The court found the witnesses had been told not to pretend illness. Upon the facts proved, it was clear that the doctor was seduced in a truly professional manner, but the court could not say as a matter of law that he was entrapped in a legal sense of that term. The court held that the defense of entrapment was not established as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order suspending the doctor's certificate for the practice of medicine and surgery.

 

1274 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Christiansen


Civ. No. 23345


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


248 Cal. App. 2d 398; 56 Cal. Rptr. 505; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1644



February 8, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County denying a guardian authorization to make gifts from the estate of an incompetent. Gerald S. Chargin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant heir challenged the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which instructed appellee guardian not to make gifts from the principal of the ward's estate, but which authorized payment from the surplus income of the estate pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 1558. The guardian, who was also appellant heir, had sought gifts for each of the adult children and grandchildren to avoid higher estate taxes upon the ward's death.


OVERVIEW: Upon petition for authorization to make gifts from the ward's estate to her children and grandchildren to effect tax savings against the estate at the time of the ward's death, the lower court found that it did not have the power to authorize gifts from the principal of the estate. But the lower court authorized a smaller gift from the surplus of the net income used for the ward's expenses. On a challenge by appellant heir, who was also appellee guardian, the court reversed the order, holding that the lower court had the authority to determine whether to authorize transfers of the ward's property to avoid unnecessary taxes or administrative expenses where it appeared that the ward, as a reasonably prudent person, would have done so had she been competent. The criteria for determining authorization included permanency of the condition, needs of the ward, devolution of the property, and donative intent. Having found sufficient evidence that the ward would have made the gifts to effect tax savings if she could have afforded to do so without prejudicing her welfare, the court remanded for findings on the amount the ward would have given based on her economic needs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, holding that a probate court had authority to determine whether to authorize gifts from the ward's estate to avoid unnecessary taxes or administrative expenses where it appeared that the ward, as a reasonably prudent person, would have done so had she been competent. The criteria for determining authorization included the condition's permanency, needs of the ward, devolution of the property, and donative intent.

Community Redevelopment Agency v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


Civ. No. 31089


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


248 Cal. App. 2d 164; 56 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1617



January 27, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 21, 1967, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. The Petition of the Real Parties in Interest for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 22, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in prohibition to restrain the Superior Court of Los Angeles County from proceeding further in an action by taxpayers to invalidate a redevelopment plan.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant redevelopment agency (agency) petitioned the court for a writ of prohibition ordering respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to desist from any further proceedings in an action after the trial court denied its motion to dismiss the action pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 863. Plaintiff taxpayers brought the action to invalidate a redevelopment plan.


OVERVIEW: The agency argued that taxpayers failed to have proper summons issued and failed to commence and complete proper publication of summons and file proof thereof within 60 days from the filing of their complaint and did not make a sufficient showing of good cause for their failure to do so. The taxpayers argued that they should not be penalized because of their attorney's failure to recognize that when he prepared and filed the complaint the procedure for testing the validity of the redevelopment plan was controlled by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 860-870 and not by the earlier sections of the law that had been repealed. The court rejected the argument that the failure constituted a mistake of law that justified a finding of good cause as required by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 863. Therefore, the court found that the taxpayers' failure to comply with § 863 required the trial court to dismiss the action on the agency's motion. The court found that where the original summons was fatally defective and no summons was published within the prescribed time, the publication of a "corrected" summons long after the expiration of the statutory 60-day period did not confer jurisdiction over the action.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining the trial court from taking any further proceedings in the action other than to enter an order dismissing the action.

People v. Hannagan


Crim. No. 11670


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


248 Cal. App. 2d 107; 56 Cal. Rptr. 429; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1609



January 25, 1967 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 24, 1967, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 22, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County and from an order sentencing appellant to state prison. Joseph L. Call, Judge.

Prosecution for violations of Health & Saf. Code, § 11501.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in which he was convicted of a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501, and sentenced to State prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was initially allowed to participate in the narcotics rehabilitation program. Later, a correction superintendent certified that defendant was not a fit subject for confinement or treatment in the rehabilitation center. The referral to the superior court was based on defendant's continuing acts of violence and apparent inability to benefit from the program. Defendant was then sentenced to State prison. Defendant contended that: (1) the trial court was without power to reassume criminal jurisdiction; (2) his rejection from the narcotics program was improper; (3) failure to give him a judicial hearing on his rejection violated due process. Defendant asked that he be returned to the superior court for commitment under the rehabilitation program or, in the alternative, that the criminal proceedings be dismissed. The court found that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3053 did not expressly provide for a certain hearing, but it had a built-in safeguard of due process rights: a court had to hold "a hearing" to reassume criminal jurisdiction and to ask whether legal cause existed. Challenges to the propriety of rejection were to be made at that time and defendant did not do so.


OUTCOME: The judgment and order sentencing defendant to State prison was affirmed.

 

1277 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Sanders v. County of Yuba


Civ. No. 11267


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


247 Cal. App. 2d 748; 55 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1732



January 17, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Yuba County. Richard A. Schoenig, Judge.

Action for damages for an injury sustained by an inmate of the county jail, and for negligence of the jailers in failing to summon medical care.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer was sustained without leave to amend reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff inmate challenged an order of the Superior Court of Yuba County (California), which sustained defendant county's demurrer in the inmate's lawsuit that alleged that the jailers were acting within the scope of their employment when they negligently failed to summon medical care for injuries that he sustained when he was struck by a metal towel rack in his cell.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed an order that sustained the county's demurrer in the inmate's negligence suit that was based on a failure to provide adequate medical care for injuries that he sustained from being struck by a metal towel rack that was in his cell. The court reversed that order on appeal and granted the inmate leave to amend his complaint. Under § 845.6 of the California Tort Claims Act of 1963 (act), Cal. Gov't. Code § 810 et seq., the county was liable for injuries that were proximately caused by the failure of its employee to obtain medical care if the employee knew or had reason to know of the inmate's need for immediate medical care and failed to summon such care. However, § 844.6 of the act provided that, notwithstanding any other provisions of law, the county was not liable for injuries to the inmate. By referring to their legislative history, the court reconciled the two sections to hold the county liable. The court also held that the power of the legislature to control public-entity immunities and tort liability was broad.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the inmate's complaint that alleged negligence by the county in failing to provide adequate medical care for injuries that he sustained after being struck by a metal towel rack and permitted the inmate to amend his complaint.

 

1278 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. Board of Directors


Civ. No. 672


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


247 Cal. App. 2d 587; 55 Cal. Rptr. 689; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1708



January 3, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County. Joseph S. Huberty, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Board of Directors of the Turlock Irrigation District to change the boundaries of the divisions of the district.



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed. Judgment granting peremptory writ modified and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant irrigation district board of directors appealed a judgment granting a peremptory writ of mandamus directing a change in the district boundaries in a mandamus proceeding initiated by plaintiff district landowners under the Cal. Water Code § 21605 in the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California).


OVERVIEW: The irrigation district board appealed a judgment which granted the landowners a peremptory writ of mandamus directing the district board to change boundaries, contending that the one man one vote doctrine was inapplicable to special districts. The court affirmed the judgment, as modified to enable the boundary changes to reflect the irrigation district board's legitimate discretion to consider factors that justified reasonable deviations in populations. The court found that the one man one vote doctrine did not apply to the special irrigation district because it did not possess any of the essential characteristics which required application of the doctrine. The court explained that the irrigation district was formed to distribute sufficient water and the accomplishment of such purposes did not require an exercise of government powers. The court nevertheless found that although the irrigation district board did have broad discretion to make changes to the district boundaries, such discretion was not unfettered. The court found that the irrigation district board abused its discretion because the population disparity was extreme and no boundary changes were made in over 30 years.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment granting the landowners a peremptory writ requiring the irrigation district board to make boundary changes in order to permit the consideration of factors which justified reasonable deviations in populations. The court affirmed the judgment as modified.

 

1279 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davis


Crim. No. 6111.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


255 Cal. App. 2d 907; 64 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1967 Cal. App. LEXIS 1355



Nov. 15, 1967 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Edward C. Olson, Judge. Appeal dismissed on motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for robbery, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. The People moved to dismiss the appeal for failure of defendant to comply with the requirement of Cal. Penal Code § 1237.5, which required that he obtain a certificate of probable cause from the trial court in order to perfect an appeal after his plea of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and two codefendants all pleaded not guilty to charges of robbery and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery and were tried jointly without objection. The two codefendants' confessions, which clearly implicated defendant as a participant in the robbery, were received into evidence with an admonition that they were not to be considered against defendant, a practice later disapproved as prejudicial. All three then changed their pleas to guilty on the robbery charge, and the kidnapping charge was dismissed. Defendant prepared and filed a notice of appeal in propria persona, which would have been timely and effective but for the provisions of § 1237.5, which required that a certificate of probable cause be obtained from the trial court. The court allowed defendant to seek the certificate from the trial court, but the trial court refused. The court then dismissed the appeal, finding that under § 1237.5 no appeal could be taken once the trial court denied the certificate. In addition, defendant's guilty plea eliminated such procedural questions as the propriety of a joint trial, and his not having sought leave to withdraw the guilty plea was another reason for dismissal.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal.

Robins v. County of L.A.


Civ. No. 30622


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


248 Cal. App. 2d 1; 56 Cal. Rptr. 853; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 928



December 21, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 5, 1967, and Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 15, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County enjoining the county and the sheriff thereof from enforcing a county ordinance requiring restaurants and bars employing "topless" waitresses was to obtain an entertainment license. Ralph H. Nutter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the county and the sheriff, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which enjoined them from enforcing a county ordinance requiring restaurants and bars employing "topless" waitresses to obtain an entertainment license, in an action brought by respondent bar owners seeking the injunction.


OVERVIEW: The bar owners operated several bars in the county where they employed topless waitresses. The county and the sheriff intended to enforce a local ordinance against the bar owners, which required them to obtain an entertainment license in order to employ topless waitresses. The bar owners sought an injunction from the trial court to enjoin the county and the sheriff from enforcing the ordinance against them, contending that they were exempt from the ordinance because they were licensed by the Alcoholic Beverage Control Board, and that the requirement violated their constitutional rights to freedom of speech and expression. The trial court granted the injunction . The county and the sheriff appealed. On appeal, the court reversed, ruling that the mere fact that the bar owners had a liquor license did not exempt them the requirement of the entertainment license, since the denial of one did not prohibit the other. There was no constitutional violation because the commercial aspects of a business enterprise were not protected interests. Finally, the bar owners were premature in seeking an injunction where they had not yet applied for or been refused the entertainment license.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, which granted injunctive relief to the bar owners.

 

1281 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


Crim. No. 5342


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


247 Cal. App. 2d 394; 55 Cal. Rptr. 550; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 977



December 21, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco and from an order granting probation with certain conditions. Norman Elkington, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with a deadly weapon.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed; order reversed in part with directions. Judgment of conviction affirmed; order granting probation reversed as to condition of making restitution.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) imposing a probation sentence for assault with a deadly weapon under Cal. Penal Code § 245a.


OVERVIEW: When defendant sought to use his credit card with a merchant, the creditor told the merchant not to return the card because defendant was unable to pay his debt. Defendant assaulted the merchant to get the card back. As a term of defendant's probation, defendant was ordered to pay the entire debt. Defendant claimed that the sentence was improper because he would have been imprisoned for the failure to pay the debt, which was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. I, § 15. The court affirmed the sentence. Because probation was given as a matter of grace by the trial court, restitution was proper under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.1 and was not unconstitutional. There was no violation of due process in the determination of how much defendant owed the creditor because defendant never disputed the debt's amount. However, the court reversed and remanded the probation condition that defendant re-pay the entire debt because the entire debt was unrelated to the assault where defendant had legally used the card, though he was unable to pay the debt. The cause was remanded for the trial court to re-consider the probation terms.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence for assault with a deadly weapon; however, the restitution condition was reversed. The cause was remanded for a review of the probation terms.

 

1282 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Frost v. State


Civ. No. 8046


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


247 Cal. App. 2d 378; 55 Cal. Rptr. 652; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 975



December 20, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 6, 1967, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 15, 1967. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Stephen K. Tamura, Judge.

Action for damages for negligence causing property damage and wrongful death arising out of an automobile accident allegedly caused by a dangerous and defective condition of a state highway.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Summary judgment of dismissal with prejudice affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the widow and minor children of the deceased, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant state. Plaintiffs' action for negligence and wrongful death arose out of an automobile accident allegedly caused by a dangerous and defective condition of the state's highway.


OVERVIEW: The state argued that the action was time-barred under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 641, 644. Plaintiffs contended that the statute of limitations was tolled by the moratorium legislation contained in the 1961 Cal. Stat. ch. 1404, § 4 and that their cause of action arose when their verified claim was rejected by the board of control. Because that rejection occurred after 1961, plaintiffs asserted that the statute of limitations did not commence until 91 days after the final adjournment of the 1963 regular session of the Legislature. Plaintiff minors contended additionally that the statute of limitations did not begin to run as to them until they had reached the age of majority. The court affirmed the state's summary judgment, holding that plaintiffs' cause of action arose on the date of death pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 377. The filing and rejection of the claim were merely conditions attached to the permission to commence the action. Cal. Gov't Code § 644 barred the cause of action six months after plaintiffs' claims were rejected because the death occurred before the 1961 legislation. Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 352 did not extend plaintiff minors' period of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of the state.

 

1283 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Department of Mental Hygiene v. Kolts


Civ. No. 30341


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


247 Cal. App. 2d 154; 55 Cal. Rptr. 437; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 949



December 12, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 8, 1967. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County dismissing an action for recovery of money expended for the care of a patient in a state hospital. Shirley M. Hufstedler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Department of Mental Hygiene of the State of California sued defendant estate, seeking to recover the sum of money expended by the state for the wife of decedent's care under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6650. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) dismissed the complaint. The Department appealed.


OVERVIEW: The husband's wife was adjudged insane and committed to a state hospital. In the judgment of commitment the husband was not ordered to pay for the care of his wife, but in a statement of financial ability, the court found that he was able to pay for the care. During his lifetime he did not pay for the wife's care at the state hospital. After the husband's death, the Department filed a verified creditor's claim for her care at the state hospital and it was rejected. The husband's estate argued that Cal. Welfare and Institutions Code violated the equal protection provision of the Constitution and that it was unconstitutional to compel anyone to pay for the care of an incompetent relative in a state mental institution. The court stated the husband's obligation to support his wife did not cease when she was adjudged insane. The court determined that the class of "husband" was not an arbitrary and unreasonable classification because the common law and other statutes codified the moral obligation of the husband to support his wife in sickness and in health. Thus, the class of "husband" was reasonably supported by rational basis and § 6650 did not deny the husband equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment.

People v. Williams


Crim. No. 2625


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


247 Cal. App. 2d 169; 55 Cal. Rptr. 434; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 951



December 12, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 8, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County and from an order denying probation. Robert W. Conyers, Judge.

Prosecution for assault with a deadly weapon.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which, following a guilty plea, convicted him of assault with a deadly weapon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), and entered a separate order denying probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to assault with a deadly weapon, admitted three prior felony convictions, and applied for probation. The trial court convicted defendant and denied probation. On review, the court affirmed. Probation was unavailable to defendant pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1203(4) due to the number of his prior convictions, but it was also unavailable because Cal. Penal Code § 1203(5) required the prosecution's consent to any offer of probation. Defendant sought to challenge the validity of the statute. The court noted, however, that the question was irrelevant because the solution of the question would not have altered defendant's status. Defendant was ineligible for probation regardless of whether or not the prosecution consented because of the previous paragraph in the statute regarding his number of prior convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court.

 

1285 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bank of America v. Department of Mental Hygiene


Civ. No. 23583


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


246 Cal. App. 2d 578; 54 Cal. Rptr. 899; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1059



November 21, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 18, 1967. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Joseph Karesh, Judge.

Action to recover money paid to state department under statute held unconstitutional.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to complaint was sustained without leave to amend affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Department of Mental Hygiene's statutory claim against plaintiff executor was settled. The statute on which the claim was based was later declared unconstitutional, and the executor filed an action to recover the amount paid in settlement. The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) entered a judgment sustaining the Department's demurrer without leave to amend, and the executor appealed.


OVERVIEW: An executor settled a claim for care rendered to its decedent's daughter and a decree was entered approving the payment and dismissing the Department's statutory action with prejudice. The executor claimed that once the statute was declared unconstitutional it had a cause of action for recovery of the settlement amount because the statute was held constitutional before the payment was made and the prior decree was not res judicata. The court held that the trial court had properly taken judicial notice of the first decree in ruling on the demurrer and that a dismissal with prejudice was res judicata of the executor's claim. Where property rights were acquired under a decision holding a statute constitutional, those rights were not invalidated by a subsequent contrary decision and in such cases the new rule was only given prospective effect. The governing law was that prevailing at the time of settlement and the later decision did not create a mistake of law that a court would rectify.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment sustaining the demurrer.

 

1286 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Douglas


Crim. No. 11344


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


246 Cal. App. 2d 594; 54 Cal. Rptr. 777; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1061



November 21, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 28, 1966, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 18, 1967. Mosk, J., and Burke, J., did not Participate Therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Alfred P. Peracca, Judge.

Prosecution for robbery and assault.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of 10 counts of robbery under Cal. Pen. Code §211, one count of assault with intent to commit murder under Cal. Pen. Code §217, and two counts of assault with a deadly weapon under Cal. Pen. Code §245.


OVERVIEW: After being tried for the murder of a police officer, defendants were tried and convicted of robbery, assault with intent to commit murder, and assault with a deadly weapon. The court affirmed defendants' convictions because double jeopardy did not attach under Cal. Penal Code § 1023 inasmuch as the prosecution could use the evidence from the murder trial as evidence in the subsequent trial for different offenses, fundamental fairness did not require the prosecution to combine all offenses into one trial because the offenses did not arise out of the same act, the same incident, or the same course of conduct, and defendants' right to a speedy trial did not require prosecution quicker than that required by the statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for robbery, assault with intent to commit murder, and assault with a deadly weapon.

 

1287 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Montenegro


Crim. No. 5618


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


246 Cal. App. 2d 515; 54 Cal. Rptr. 865; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1050



November 18, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody.



DISPOSITION: Maximum term of imprisonment declared; order to show cause discharged and writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a proceeding in habeas corpus to secure his release from custody. Petitioner had pleaded guilty to possession of heroin and admitted a prior misdemeanor conviction under a former "drug addict statute," Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721 (1955). The prior conviction increased his maximum term from 10 years to 20 years, and he asserted that his conviction was under that portion of § 11721 that was since held unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: An information filed in connection with the conviction charged that petitioner and another violated § 11721 in that they "did unlawfully use and are addicted to" narcotics, and were under the influence thereof. To the extent that it provided punishment for addiction to narcotics, the United States Supreme Court held the statute unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that (1) petitioner had the burden of showing that his conviction rested upon the unconstitutional, rather than the valid, portion of the statute; (2) although a plea of guilty to the conjunctive charge would have admitted the use of narcotics in addition to addiction, the case was tried to a jury; (3) the jury instructions reflected that the trial court read the conjunctive charge as given in the information, but it later read disjunctive language of the statute itself, which permitted the jury to convict petitioner for addiction; (4) because no transcript was available, the unconstitutional part of the statute was a possible alternative sole sufficient ground of conviction; and (5) an attack made by petitioner upon the conviction itself was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the term for which petitioner was imprisoned has a maximum of 10 years. The order to show cause was discharged because petitioner was not then entitled to release.

 

1288 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Halko


Crim. No. 12263


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


246 Cal. App. 2d 553; 54 Cal. Rptr. 661; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1055



November 18, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from the security side of a hospital.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner patient sought a writ of habeas corpus for his release from the security side of respondent hospital where he was being held pursuant to consecutive certificates of quarantine and isolation pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 3285.


OVERVIEW: The patient was diagnosed with pulmonary tuberculosis and was served with a quarantine order of isolation confining him to the hospital. When he deserted the hospital, he was convicted of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 3351. Prior to serving his jail sentence the patient was served with an order of isolation because of his tubercular condition and was returned to the security side of the hospital The patient sought a writ of habeas corpus for his release from the hospital contending that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 3285 was unconstitutional. The court denied the writ. The provisions of § 3285 were reasonable and necessary for the protection of the public health. Moreover, § 3285 did not contain any limitation or prohibition respecting the period of quarantine or the power of the health officer to issue consecutive certificates of isolation. The law reasonably assumed that consecutive orders for quarantine were allowed because there was nothing to indicate that the health officer issued any of the respective quarantine orders without probable cause or that the patient did not have an infectious and communicable disease which was dangerous to the public health.


OUTCOME: The court denied the patient's petition for writ of habeas corpus for his release from the security side of the hospital.

Stewart v. County of San Mateo


Civ. Nos. 23062, 23457


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


246 Cal. App. 2d 273; 54 Cal. Rptr. 599; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1025



November 9, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Application of Plaintiff and Appellant, and of Petitioner, for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 4, 1967. Sullivan, J., did not Participate Therein. Traynor, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL (No. 23062) from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County. W. Howard Hartley, Judge. Consolidated with PROCEEDING (No. 23457) in prohibition to restrain the Municipal Court of the Central Judicial District of San Mateo County from proceeding in a criminal action.



DISPOSITION: Judgment (No. 23062) affirmed. Alternative writ (No. 23457) discharged and peremptory writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff investigator appealed from the decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which rejected his request for a writ of prohibition to prevent defendants, county and municipal court, from enforcing a county ordinance or taking criminal action under it. Under the ordinance, the investigator's permit for a private patrol service had been revoked.


OVERVIEW: The investigator sought declaratory relief to determine the validity of County of San Mateo, Cal., Ordinance § 5620.8. He sought to restrain the municipal court from proceeding further in a criminal action brought against him upon a complaint alleging that he violated the ordinance by operating his private patrol service during a certain time period. The court affirmed the superior court's decision, holding that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7523 permitted local regulation of private patrol operators, and it was not an unconstitutional delegation of state legislative authority. California, by virtue of its enactment of the Private Investigator and Adjuster Act § 7500 et seq. had not preempted the field of disciplinary action with respect to private patrol operators so as to make unlawful such a local ordinance providing for revocation of a private patrol operator's permit to operate in the county. Further the permit revocation procedure provided for in San Mateo County, Cal., Ordinance § 5620.8 did not violate procedural due process under either the federal or state constitutions. Thus, the investigator was not entitled to the requested writ of prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court denied the investigator's request for a writ of prohibition declaring invalid a county ordinance under which his permit for a private patrol service was revoked.

 

1290 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Gonzales


Crim. No. 2606


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


246 Cal. App. 2d 296; 54 Cal. Rptr. 689; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1027



November 9, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 4, 1967. Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to secure release from custody after commitment to the state narcotic rehabilitation facility. William A. Munnell and Arthur K. Marshall, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted and petitioner remanded to the custody of the Los Angeles County sheriff for further proceedings pending before the Municipal Court of Whittier Judicial District of Los Angeles County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to set aside the civil commitment order entered by the Municipal Court of Whittier Judicial District (California), which committed him to a state narcotic rehabilitation facility as a narcotic addict, purportedly pursuant to the provisions of former Cal. Penal Code § 6450.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to set aside his commitment to a state rehabilitation center as a narcotic addict. He was not served with a certified copy of the order fixing the time and place of the hearing culminating in the commitment, as was prescribed by former Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5353. At the time and place set for the hearing, petitioner and his counsel appeared; a hearing was conducted; the trial court found petitioner to be a narcotic addict; and the commitment order followed. The court on appeal granted the writ. Jurisdiction to enter an order of civil commitment to the narcotic addict rehabilitation program depended on strict compliance with each of the specific statutory prerequisites for maintenance of the proceeding. The requirements respecting notice of hearing fell within that category. Under the rule stated, as there was not strict compliance with each of the specific statutory prerequisites for maintenance of the proceeding in petitioner's case, the proceeding was fatally defective; the trial court did not have jurisdiction in the premises to commit; and the commitment order was void.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's writ of habeas corpus was granted and petitioner was ordered discharged from custody of the narcotic rehabilitation facility, he was remanded to the custody of the sheriff for further proceedings before the trial court as was appropriate.

 

1291 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Valverde


Crim. No. 277


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


246 Cal. App. 2d 318; 54 Cal. Rptr. 528; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1029



November 9, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 4, 1967. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County and from an order denying motion for a new trial. Bill L. Dozier, Judge.

Prosecution for the illegal sale of heroin.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed; appeal from order dismissed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was convicted of selling drugs by the Superior Court of San Joaquin County, (California), appealed his conviction, and an order of the trial court denying defendant's motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly sold drugs to a drug addict who worked undercover for the police. Defendant claimed that the trial court erroneously imposed upon defendant the burden of proving the defense of entrapment by a preponderance of the evidence. The court held that entrapment did not go to the question of guilt or innocence. The availability of the defense depended upon whether the intent to commit the crime originated in the mind of defendant or in the mind of the entrapping officer, and where a defendant had a preexisting criminal intent, the fact that when solicited by a decoy he committed a crime did not show entrapment. Viewing the evidence of entrapment in the light most favorable to defendant, it contained nothing going to the elements of the crime. The record could not have been construed to negate defendant's intent to make the several sales charged. At most, it showed that this intent was induced by acts of law enforcement officers. Defendant had the burden of persuasion or the burden of proof by a preponderance of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment. The order appealed from was dismissed.

Los Robles Motor Lodge, Inc. v. Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control


Civ. No. 11370


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


246 Cal. App. 2d 198; 54 Cal. Rptr. 547; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1019



November 7, 1966

PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Charles W. Johnson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control to set aside an order suspending a liquor license.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) which denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate to vacate the order which granted judgment in favor of defendants in an action alleging violations of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25601 and suspending appellant's liquor license.


OVERVIEW: Appellee state alcohol commission suspended appellant's liquor license after allegations that appellant facilitated prostitutes in appellant's bar and permitted the motel to be used as a disorderly house. The trial court refused to issue a writ of mandate after appellant received adverse decisions from all administrative agencies because the findings were supported by substantial evidence. Appellant raised that issue on appeal and that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25601 was void for vagueness. Section 25601 was neither void for vagueness nor violative of due process but was, on the contrary, a valid exercise of the state's police power. The decision of the appellee administrative agency was supported by substantial evidence because the investigators provided detailed information of all the facts relating to the allegations of permitting the motel to be used as a disorderly house.


OUTCOME: Judgment finding substantial evidence supported the agency action of suspending appellant's liquor license upheld, because the investigators provided detailed information of all the facts relating to the allegations of permitting the motel to be used as a disorderly house.

Elser v. Gill Net Number One


Civ. Nos. 23058, 23059


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


246 Cal. App. 2d 30; 54 Cal. Rptr. 568; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1843



October 31, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied December 28, 1966. Mosk, J., did not Participate Therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Humboldt County. William G. Watson, Jr., Judge.

Actions to forfeit defendant gill nets.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments denying forfeiture affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff fish and game commissioners challenged two decisions of the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California), which refused to forfeit defendant gill nets. Intervenor Indians had claimed ownership of the nets in question. The two actions were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: The gill nets had been seized for exceeding the permitted mesh size. Both Indians were born and had lived and fished most of their lives in the area where the nets were found. On appeal, the commissioners argued that the trial court improperly concluded that the Indians were exempt from the provisions of the Fish and Game Code pursuant to Cal. Fish & Game Code § 12300. The court affirmed the judgments denying forfeiture, holding that the trial court had correctly found that the Indians were exempt under § 12300. The court held that the trial court properly found that the Indians were full-blooded Yurok or Lower Klamath River Indians, descendants of Lower Klamath River Indians who were allotted specific tracts of land in the Hoopa Extension. The court also found that both were enrolled as Klamath River or Yurok Indians and as "wards" of the federal government on the 1931, 1937, and 1940 rolls of the Bureau of Indian Affairs (Bureau), as well as the Bureau's roll of living allottees and direct descendants of allottees on the Hoopa Extension. The court did not agree with the commissioner's contention that the requirement of living in a "tribal relation" must be read into § 21300.


OUTCOME: The judgments denying forfeiture of the gill nets in favor of the Indians were affirmed.

 

1294 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Department of Mental Hygiene v. O'Connor


Civ. No. 23764


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


246 Cal. App. 2d 24; 54 Cal. Rptr. 432; 1966 Cal. App. LEXIS 1001



October 31, 1966 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco. George E. Maloney, Judge.

Action by the Department of Mental Hygiene against the administrator of an estate for costs of care furnished decedent's wife as a patient in a state mental institution.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment for defendant reversed with directions to enter judgment on the pleadings for plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a state's department of mental hygiene, sought review of a judgment from the Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which was in favor of defendant estate administrator in an action for costs of care furnished to the decedent's wife as a patient in a state mental institution.


OVERVIEW: The administrator denied liability by contending that precedent rendered unconstitutional the portions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6650 that provided for the liability which the department sought to impose. The case in question had provided that an adult child could not be charged for costs incurred by her mentally ill parent while the latter was a patient in a state mental hospital. Thus, on appeal of the judgment for the administrator, the issue was whether the estate of the deceased husband was liable on the department's claim for the care furnished to the decedent's deceased wife as a patient in a state mental institution during their joint married life. Upon its examination of the relevant precedent, the court determined that the case stood for the proposition that no person or class of persons were to be charged with support where no rational basis would underlie the classification. Given that the state had a legitimate interest in a husband's support of his wife, the department had a right to seek reimbursement from the husband, as he was otherwise legally responsible for the care, support, and maintenance of his wife while she was treated at the facility.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for the administrator with directions to enter judgment on the pleadings for the department.
